<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="42003"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="De novo genome assembly of Oryza granulata reveals rapid genome expansion and adaptive evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Communications Biology 2018 1:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-06-29"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The wild relatives of rice have adapted to different ecological environments and constitute a useful reservoir of agronomic traits for genetic improvement. Here we present the ~777&#8201;Mb de novo assembled genome sequence of Oryza granulata. Recent bursts of long-terminal repeat retrotransposons, especially RIRE2, led to a rapid twofold increase in genome size after O. granulata speciation. Universal centromeric tandem repeats are absent within its centromeres, while gypsy-type LTRs constitute the main centromere-specific repetitive elements. A total of 40,116 protein-coding genes were predicted in O. granulata, which is close to that of Oryza sativa. Both the copy number and function of genes involved in photosynthesis and energy production have undergone positive selection during the evolution of O. granulata, which might have facilitated its adaptation to the low light habitats. Together, our findings reveal the rapid genome expansion, distinctive centromere organization, and adaptive evolution of O. granulata. Zhigang Wu, Dongming Fang, Rui Yang, et al. present the genome assembly of a wild rice species Oryza granulata, revealing critical insights about the rapid genome expansion and evolution observed in the Oryza genus. They find that recent bursts of LTR retrotransposons have led to the rapid increase in O. granulate genome size following speciation."/>

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8428-8010"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-8428-8010</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Chinese Academy of Sciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000000119573309, grid.9227.e, State Key Laboratory of Plant Genomics, Institute of Genetics and Developmental Biology, Chinese Academy of Sciences, 100101, Beijing, China" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Qiong-Luo" data-author-popup="auth-Qiong-Luo" data-corresp-id="c3">Qiong Luo<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Yunnan Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.410696.c, State Key Laboratory for Conservation and Utilization of Bio-Resources in Yunnan, Ministry of Education Key Laboratory of Agriculture Biodiversity for Plant Disease Management, Yunnan Agricultural University, 650201, Kunming, China" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The wild relatives of rice have adapted to different ecological environments and constitute a useful reservoir of agronomic traits for genetic improvement. Here we present the ~777 Mb de novo assembled genome sequence of <i>Oryza granulata</i>. Recent bursts of long-terminal repeat retrotransposons, especially <i>RIRE2</i>, led to a rapid twofold increase in genome size after <i>O. granulata</i> speciation. Universal centromeric tandem repeats are absent within its centromeres, while <i>gypsy</i>-type LTRs constitute the main centromere-specific repetitive elements. A total of 40,116 protein-coding genes were predicted in <i>O. granulata</i>, which is close to that of <i>Oryza sativa</i>. Both the copy number and function of genes involved in photosynthesis and energy production have undergone positive selection during the evolution of <i>O. granulata</i>, which might have facilitated its adaptation to the low light habitats. Together, our findings reveal the rapid genome expansion, distinctive centromere organization, and adaptive evolution of <i>O. granulata</i>.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Rapid advancements in sequencing technology have facilitated comparative genomics based on fully sequenced genomes for studying gene and genome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Alfoldi, J. &amp; Lindblad.-Toh, K. Comparative genomics as a tool to understand evolution and disease. Genome Res. 23, 1063–1068 (2013)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39817e536">1</a></sup>. In plants, whole-genome duplication, sequence reorganization, and transposable element (TE) proliferation have complicated genome organization greatly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Soltis, P. S., Marchant, D. B., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Polyploidy and genome evolution in plants. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 35, 119–125 (2015)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39817e540">2</a></sup>. Even within closely related species, such as those in the genus <i>Oryza</i> or <i>Arabidopsis</i>, remarkable fluctuation in gene collinearity and genome size has been observed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Piegu, B. et al. Doubling genome size without polyploidization: dynamics of retrotransposition- driven genomic expansions in Oryza australiensis, a wild relative of rice. Genome Res. 16, 1262–1269 (2006)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39817e550">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, J. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of Oryza brachyantha reveals mechanisms underlying Oryza genome evolution. Nat. Commun. 4, 1595 (2013)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39817e550_1">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hu, T. T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d39817e553">5</a></sup>. Comparative analysis of orthologous regions or whole-genomic sequences within closely related species, especially within the same genus, may shed more light on the mechanism of genome evolution.</p><p>The genus <i>Oryza</i> contains 24 species, including two cultivated and 22 wild species, distributed throughout the world. Based on morphological, cytological, and molecular divergence studies, they have been classified into ten genome types, namely AA, BB, CC, BBCC, CCDD, EE, FF, GG, HHJJ, and HHKK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ge, S., Sang, T., Lu, B. R., &amp; Hong, D. Y. Phylogeny of rice genomes with emphasis on origins of allotetraploid species. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 96, 14400–14405 (1999)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d39817e563">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Zou, X. H. et al. Analysis of 142 genes resolves the rapid diversification of the rice genus. Genome Biol. 9, R49 (2008)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d39817e566">7</a></sup>. The two cultivated species, <i>O. sativa</i> (Asian cultivar) and <i>Oryza glaberrima</i> (African cultivar) both with AA genome, have been extensively sequenced and studied in the past years, because of their outstanding contribution to world food security<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Matsumoto, T. et al. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39817e576">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Du, H. L. et al. Sequencing and de novo assembly of a near complete indica rice genome. Nat. Commun. 8, 15324 (2017)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d39817e576_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Wang, M. et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat. Genet. 46, 982–988 (2014)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d39817e579">10</a></sup>. The wild rice genomes not only constitute a useful reservoir of genetic diversity, contributing important genetic resources for rice breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Vaughan, D. A., Morishima, H. &amp; Kadowaki, K. Diversity in the Oryza genus. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 6, 139–146 (2003)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d39817e584">11</a></sup>, but also provide fundamental data for studying plant genome evolution within a short timeframe. However, only two types of wild rice genomes, the AA and FF genomes, have been published thus far<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Chen, J. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of Oryza brachyantha reveals mechanisms underlying Oryza genome evolution. Nat. Commun. 4, 1595 (2013)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39817e588">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zhang, Q. J. et al. Rapid diversification of five Oryza AA genomes associated with rice adaptation. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4954–E4962 (2014)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d39817e591">12</a></sup>. The wild rice <i>O. granulata</i> possesses a GG genome and has been placed within the basal lineage of the genus <i>Oryza</i> with the second largest genome among the diploid rice species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Zou, X. H. et al. Analysis of 142 genes resolves the rapid diversification of the rice genus. Genome Biol. 9, R49 (2008)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d39817e601">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ammiraju, J. S. et al. The Oryza bacterial artificial chromosome library resource: construction and analysis of 12 deep-coverage large-insert BAC libraries that represent the 10 genome types of the genus. Oryza. Genome Res. 16, 140–147 (2006)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39817e604">13</a></sup>. Its genome is dramatically expanded, with a genome size twice that of <i>O. sativa</i>, and contains a different set of repeat sequences relative to other <i>Oryza</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lee, H. R. et al. Chromatin immunoprecipitation cloning reveals rapid evolutionary patterns of centromeric DNA in Oryza species. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 102, 11793–11798 (2005)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39817e615">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Zuccolo, A. et al. Transposable element distribution, abundance and role in genome size variation in the genus Oryza. BMC Evol. Biol. 7, 152 (2007)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39817e618">15</a></sup>. Furthermore, <i>O. granulata</i> also has several distinctive natural traits, as it is photoperiod-insensitive and tolerant to shade<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Vaughan, D. A. The Wild Relatives of Rice: A Genetic Resources Handbook 40−42 (International Rice Research Institute, Manila, Philippines, 1994)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d39817e625">16</a></sup>. Therefore, comparative analyses of <i>O. granulata</i> with other sequenced <i>Oryza</i> genomes provide a unique opportunity to explore genomic size variation and adaptive evolution in this genus.</p><p>Centromeres are chromosomal sites for kinetochore assembly and spindle attachment, thereby enabling accurate chromosome segregation during each cell cycle. The presence of CENH3, a centromere-specific histone H3 variant, is the hallmark of all functional centromeres<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="McKinley, K. L. &amp; Cheeseman, I. M. The molecular basis for centromere identity and function. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Bio. 17, 16 (2016)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d39817e638">17</a></sup>. In most animals and plants that have been studied, centromeres are composed of long arrays of satellite repeats and/or long-terminal repeats (LTRs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jiang, J., Birchler, J. A., Parrott, W. A. &amp; Dawe, R. K. A molecular view of plant centromeres. Trends Plant. Sci. 8, 570–575 (2003)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39817e642">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Melters, D. P. et al. Comparative analysis of tandem repeats from hundreds of species reveals unique insights into centromere evolution. Genome Biol. 14, R10 (2013)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39817e645">19</a></sup>. Centromere functions are conserved among all eukaryotes, yet their sequences evolve rapidly and show tremendous variation both in size and organization pattern, even between closely related species and different chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Melters, D. P. et al. Comparative analysis of tandem repeats from hundreds of species reveals unique insights into centromere evolution. Genome Biol. 14, R10 (2013)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39817e649">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Gong, Z. Y. et al. Repeatless and repeat-based centromeres in potato: implications for centromere evolution. Plant Cell 24, 3559–3574 (2012)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d39817e652">20</a></sup>. Cultivated rice and its wild relatives provide an excellent system for exploring centromeric sequences evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Yan, H. H. &amp; Jiang, J. M. Rice as a model for centromere and heterochromatin research. Chromosome Res. 15, 77–84 (2007)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d39817e656">21</a></sup>. Centromeres of <i>O. sativa</i> are mainly composed of a 155-bp satellite repeat CentO and CRR (Centromeric Retrotransposon of Rice) elements, which both are closely intermingled<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Cheng, Z. K. et al. Functional rice centromeres are marked by a satellite repeat and a centromere-specific retrotransposon. Plant Cell 14, 1691–1704 (2002)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39817e664">22</a></sup>. CRR is derived from a <i>Ty3/gypsy</i> class of retrotransposon and the amount of CentO in each centromere varies from ~60 kb to ~2 Mb. A recent study reported that <i>Gran3</i> retrotransposons are the functional centromere elements of <i>O. granulata</i>; however, FISH signals of <i>Gran3</i> are not restricted to centromeric regions but dispersed across both the centromeric and pericentromeric regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Yi, C. D. et al. Isolation and identification of a functional centromere element in the wild rice species Oryza granulata with the GG Genome. J. Genet. Genom. 42, 699–702 (2015)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39817e680">23</a></sup>. Thus, the centromere-specific elements of <i>O. granulata</i> remain enigmatic.</p><p>In this study, we use single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing approach combined with a fosmid library to assemble ~777 Mb of the <i>O. granulata</i> genome. <i>O. granulata</i> has an expanded genome, nearly double that of <i>O. sativa</i>, composed of more than 67% of repeat elements. We show that recent bursting of LTRs, especially <i>RIRE2</i>, <i>ATLANTYS</i>, and <i>Copia</i>, contribute greatly to the rapid expansion of the <i>O. granulata</i> genome. No tandem repeats were screened out in the centromeric sequence library, demonstrating that the centromeres of <i>O. granulata</i> are lack of tandem repeats. We annotated 40,116 genes in <i>O. granulata</i>, which is comparable to that of <i>O. sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ammiraju, J. S. et al. The Oryza bacterial artificial chromosome library resource: construction and analysis of 12 deep-coverage large-insert BAC libraries that represent the 10 genome types of the genus. Oryza. Genome Res. 16, 140–147 (2006)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39817e721">13</a></sup>. Both the copy number and function of genes involved in photosynthesis and energy production have undergone positive selection, which would facilitate the adaptation of <i>O. granulata</i> to its low light habitats.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">De novo assembly and annotation of the <i>O. granulata</i> genome</h3><p>We sequenced <i>O. granulata</i> genomic DNA to generate 20.58 Gb (~26×) pair-end (2 × 150 bp), 79.51 Gb (~102×) pair-end (2 × 250 bp) reads using Illumina GA II platform, and 16.6 Gb (~21×) PacBio single-molecule long reads (with 8.7 Kb average length) from 15 cells using P5 polymerase binding and C3 chemistry (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">1</a>). We also sequenced 384 fosmid pools, each containing <span class="stix">∼</span> 1000 independent clones with insert size of <span class="stix">∼</span>40 kb, to produce 5.06 Gb of GBS tags of length 2 × 150 bp (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><p>We performed a PacBio-only assembly using an overlap-layout-consensus method implemented in Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39817e757">24</a></sup>. Due to the small size of DNA libraries, short read lengths of Illumina sequencing, and high level of repeat sequences, the Illumina-only assembly was highly fragmented and not complete using four other assembly methods: SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d39817e761">25</a></sup>, BASE, DiscoverDeNovo, and MaSuRCA. Based on contig N50 results, SOAPdenovo2 long nonredundant contigs were selected and added to the PacBio-only assembly. Subsequently, Illumina short reads were mapped onto the assembly using BWA (keeping only unique alignments with mapping quality &gt;20). Then, GATK was used to call variants, including SNPs and indels. We corrected homozygous SNPs and indels with at least five supporting reads. Finally, the custom tool Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d39817e765">26</a></sup> was used to polish the assembly and correct remaining indel errors. The final assembled sequence was 776.96 Mb with a contig N50 size of 262 kb (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">2</a>), which is nearly twice that of <i>O. sativa</i>, and by far the largest of the published <i>Oryza</i> genomes. The genome size is consistent with <i>K</i>-mer analysis from short reads estimated at ~785 Mb (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">1</a> and Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">3</a>), and a previous study by flow cytometry estimated at 882 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ammiraju, J. S. et al. The Oryza bacterial artificial chromosome library resource: construction and analysis of 12 deep-coverage large-insert BAC libraries that represent the 10 genome types of the genus. Oryza. Genome Res. 16, 140–147 (2006)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39817e788">13</a></sup>.</p><p>To assess the coverage and completeness of the genome assembly, we generated low-coverage (~6×) mate-pair reads from a ~40 kb fosmid library. Most (~98.90%) of the mate-pair reads were unambiguously mapped onto the final assembly (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">1</a>), exhibiting a peak at ~34 kb (~23.84%) mapping distance (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">2</a>). In addition, using RNA sequencing data generated from the root, stem, sheath, panicle, and leaf tissues of <i>O. granulata</i>, 92.70–93.46% of the reads could be mapped onto the final assembled sequence (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">4</a>). Additional quality analysis indicated that 95.16% of the core eukaryotic genes (CEGMA) were present in our <i>O. granulata</i> assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39817e811">27</a></sup>, 97.98% were partially represented (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">2</a>). For further assessment of the assembly and annotation completeness, we used the software tool BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs), which indicated that 95% of the set consists of complete single-copy BUSCOs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39817e818">28</a></sup> (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">2</a>), suggesting near completeness of euchromatin components. Taken together, these results indicate that our genome assembly is of high quality.</p><p>By combining ab initio RNA-seq reads and plant genes and domains, we predicted that the <i>O. granulata</i> genome contains 40,116 protein-coding genes (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">2</a>), of which 84.05% are supported by at least ten RNA-seq reads obtained from five <i>O. granulata</i> tissues (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">3</a>). Moreover, of the inferred proteins, 33,901 match entries in the SWISS-PROT, InterPro, KEGG, or TrEMBL databases (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">5</a>). We also annotated 2038 ncRNAs, including 1083 tRNAs, 722 rRNAs, and 233 microRNAs (miRNAs) encoding genes (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat sequences in <i>O. granulata</i>
                        </h3><p>Approximately 67.96% (~528.04 Mb) of the <i>O. granulata</i> genome is composed of repetitive sequences, which is much higher than that determined for <i>O. sativa</i> (~45.52%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Matsumoto, T. et al. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39817e865">8</a></sup>, indicating that expansion of repeat sequences occurred in <i>O. granulata</i> (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">7</a>). As a result, <i>O. granulata</i> has 358.12 Mb more repetitive sequences than <i>O. sativa</i>, which is the major determinant of genome expansion in <i>O. granulata</i>. Comparative analysis of high-frequency <i>K</i>-mers from the short reads of the <i>O. granulata</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> genomes indicated that there are more specific <i>K</i>-mers in the <i>O. granulata</i> genome relative to <i>O. sativa</i>. However, very few common <i>K</i>-mers were identified (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">8</a>), suggesting that there is a large difference in the composition of repeat sequences between the two genomes. These results indicate that repeat sequences in the <i>O. granulata</i> genome evolved in a distinctive way in the genus <i>Oryza</i>.</p><p>Retrotransposons, especially long-terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons, are the most abundant repeat elements in <i>O. granulata</i>, accounting for ~59.33% of the <i>O. granulata</i> genome (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">9</a>). Aside from LTRs, annotated tandem repeats and DNA transposons represent only 2.42 and 8.80% of the genome, respectively (Supplementary Tables&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">7</a> and&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">9</a>). A total of 250 LTR retrotransposon families (masked size &gt;500 Kb) were identified in <i>O. granulata</i>, including 39 and 200 members of the <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> families, respectively. <i>Gypsy</i>-type LTRs, one of the dominant forms of repeat elements found in plant genomes, are the most abundant subtype present in <i>O. granulata</i>, accounting for ~407.04 Mb of the genome (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">10</a>). In addition to <i>Gypsy</i>-type LTRs, <i>Copia</i>-type LTRs account for ~54.90 Mb of the genome (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">10</a>). In contrast, <i>Gypsy</i>- and <i>Copia</i>-type LTRs are only comprised 83.48 and 20.10 Mb in <i>O. sativa</i>, respectively (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">10</a>). These data indicate that <i>Gypsy</i>-type LTRs are five times more abundant in <i>O. granulata</i> relative to <i>O. sativa</i>, suggesting that bursts of <i>Gypsy</i>-type LTRs might be responsible for the increased genome size observed in <i>O. granulata</i>.</p><p>Further analysis of <i>Gypsy</i>- and <i>Copia</i>-type LTR families indicated that three subtypes, namely <i>RIRE2</i>, <i>ATLANTYS</i>, and <i>Copia</i>, are significantly expanded in <i>O. granulata</i> as compared with those of <i>O. sativa</i> (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01). Furthermore, <i>RIRE2</i>, <i>ATLANTYS</i>, and <i>Copia</i> subtypes make up approximately 133.18 Mb (i.e. 17.14%), 48.16 Mb (i.e. 6.20%), and 47.35 Mb (i.e. 6.09%), respectively, of the genome of <i>O. granulata</i> when considered separately (Supplementary Tables&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">11</a> and&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">12</a>). In contrast, these three LTR subtypes only contribute 5.97 Mb (i.e. 1.60%), 8.68 Mb (i.e. 2.33%), and 10.85 Mb (i.e. 2.91%), respectively, to the <i>O. sativa</i> genome, suggesting that the amplification of these typical LTRs, especially <i>RIRE2</i>, plays a dominant role in the expansion of repeat sequences in <i>O. granulata</i>.</p><p>The <i>RIRE2</i>-LTR is a 441 bp DNA segment, which was first identified as a Gypsy-type retrotransposon in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Ohtsubo, H., Kumekawa, N. &amp; Ohtsubo, E. RIRE2, a novel gypsy-type retrotransposon from rice. Genes. Genet. Syst. 74, 83–91 (1999)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1054">29</a></sup>. Typically, <i>RIRE2</i> has a long internal region that is almost 10 kb long (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">4</a>), which contains two long open reading frames (ORF1 and ORF2) encoding the <i>gag</i>, <i>pro</i>, <i>rt</i>, <i>rh</i>, and <i>int</i> genes in that order. In the <i>O. granulata</i> genome, 70% of <i>RIRE2-</i>LTRs have intact ORFs, and the remaining 30% have been partially deleted, which can be illustrated by plotting the coverage of each ORF along each <i>RIRE2</i> sequence. Moreover, by plotting the positions of <i>RIRE2</i> insertions and comparing them to those of the <i>O. sativa</i> genome, we found that <i>RIRE2</i> is frequently inserted into the regions surrounding centromeres (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#Fig1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs42003-018-0089-4/MediaObjects/42003_2018_89_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs42003-018-0089-4/MediaObjects/42003_2018_89_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="343"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Characterization of 1597 collinear gene blocks between <i>O. granulata</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> genome. Chromosome plots of the <i>O. sativa</i> genome (left), comprising 1019 contigs with collinear gene blocks of <i>O. granulata</i> (right) that cover ~45.86 % of the assembly size. Chromosomes and contigs filled with density of repeat elements for every 100 kb window. Syntenic regions are shaded in gray boxes between them. Black circles show the percentage masked by <i>RIRE2</i> in a window, and only where the proportion is more than 0.2 here</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Evolutionary history of repeat sequences in <i>O. granulata</i>
                        </h3><p>Our data indicated that <i>RIRE2</i>, <i>ATLANTYS</i>, and <i>Copia</i> LTRs are remarkably expanded in <i>O. granulata</i>, contributing a total of 228 Mb to the genome. By homology searching with sequences of <i>RIRE2</i>, <i>ATLANTYS</i>, and <i>Copia</i> LTRs, we found that all the three LTRs are also present in <i>Oryza brachyantha</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> (Supplementary Tables&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">11</a> and&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">12</a>), suggesting an ancient origin of these LTRs in the genus <i>Oryza</i>. In order to characterize and date the transposition burst of these elements in <i>O. granulata</i>, we conducted phylogenetic analyses of the complete element conservation (supported by conserved region more than 90% coverage) in the three <i>Oryza</i> species (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">5</a>). Our data showed that paralogs of the elements found in each <i>Oryza</i> species form a cluster, which is distinct from those found in the other two <i>Oryza</i> species, suggesting that the retrotransposition bursts either occurred concomitantly or after the speciation of <i>O. granulata</i>.</p><p>In order to test this hypothesis, we estimated the dates of retrotransposition of these three LTR subtypes in the <i>O. granulata</i> genome. In the case of full-length LTR retrotransposons, the date of insertion can be estimated based on the divergence between these two LTRs. The results showed that these LTRs have different transpositional activities. <i>RIRE2</i> showed a recent burst at 0.1–0.4 MYA (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#Fig2">2a</a>), while <i>ATLANTYS</i> and <i>Copia</i> showed relatively earlier bursts at 0.5–0.8 and 1.0–2.0 MYA, respectively (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#Fig2">2b</a>). Our data indicated that these elements expanded more recently, and occurred much later than the divergence date of <i>O. granulata</i> species. Thus, we propose that this repeat-driven genomic expansion is posterior to and not concomitant with speciation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs42003-018-0089-4/MediaObjects/42003_2018_89_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs42003-018-0089-4/MediaObjects/42003_2018_89_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="363"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Timing of the bursts of the three retrotransposons, <i>RIRE2</i>, <i>ATLANTYS</i>, and <i>COPIA</i>, in <i>O. granulata</i>. <b>a</b> Timing of the burst of <i>RIRE2</i>. <b>b</b> Timing of the burst of <i>ATLANTYS</i> and <i>COPIA</i>. For each LTR retrotransposons in <i>O. granulata</i> genome, the curves represent the date of divergence in MYA translated from the observed divergence, using the molecular clock (1.3×10<sup>−</sup><sup>8</sup> substitutions per site per year from Ma and Bennetzen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Ma, J. X. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1274">47</a></sup>). The groups of paralogs used to compute the pairwise distances are defined with EMBOSS. The <i>y</i>-axis represents the total number of copy equivalents</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">GG genome centromeres lack tandem repeats</h3><p>Centromeres usually contain an abundance of repetitive sequences, including tandem repeats and retrotransposons. However, tandem repeats were not found in centromeric regions of <i>O. granulata</i> chromosomes by FISH assays. In order to identify the centromeric elements of <i>O. granulata</i>, ChIP cloning was carried out using a rice anti-CENH3 antibody that showed high specificity to <i>O. granulata</i> centromeres (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#Fig3">3a</a>). Using the ChIP DNAs as FISH probes, we found that they are highly enriched in centromeric regions of <i>O. granulata</i> chromosomes (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#Fig3">3b</a>). In order to obtain more details of the centromeric elements in <i>O. granulata</i>, a plasmid library of ChIP DNAs was generated, and 34 clones with an insert size larger than 1000 bp were randomly selected as FISH probes. Probes with positive FISH signals in the centromeric regions of <i>O. granulata</i> chromosomes were perceived as Granulata Centromere Sequence (GCS). GCS1 was the first identified probe that showed unambiguous magenta FISH signals in the centromeric regions of 12 <i>O. granulata</i> chromosomes, flanked by obvious cyan FISH signals of pericentromeric probe <i>RIRE2</i> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#Fig3">3c</a>). We also identified additional four GCSs, including GCS2, GCS3, GCS4, and GCS5, were localized in the centromeric regions of <i>O. granulata</i> chromosomes (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">6</a>). Nevertheless, Immuno-FISH experiments confirmed that <i>RIRE2</i> was mainly located in pericentromeric regions but not in centromeric regions of <i>O. granulata</i> chromosomes (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#Fig3">3d</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs42003-018-0089-4/MediaObjects/42003_2018_89_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs42003-018-0089-4/MediaObjects/42003_2018_89_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="830"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>FISH mapping of centromeric and pericentromeric probes on pachytene chromosomes of <i>O. granulata</i> and phenotypic characterization of its pachytene chromosomes. <b>a</b> Immunodetection of rabbit antibody against rice CENH3 on pachytene chromosomes of <i>O. granulata</i>. <b>b</b> FISH identification of precipitated DNA isolated by ChIP using rice anti-CENH3 antibodies. <b>c</b> Dual color FISH of GCS1 cloned from ChIP DNAs and <i>RIRE2</i>. GCS1 was labeled with digoxigenin-dUTP (magenta), while <i>RIRE2</i> was labeled with biotin-dUTP (cyan). <b>d</b> Immuno-FISH detection of <i>RIRE2</i>. Rice anti-CENH3 antibodies were used to label centromeres. <b>e</b> Pachytene chromosome morphology of <i>O. granulata</i>. Arrows indicate the 12 centromere constrictions on each pachytene chromosome of <i>O. granulata</i>. <b>f</b> Pachytene chromosome morphology of <i>O. sativa</i>. Chromosomes were counterstained with DAPI. Scale bars = 5 μm</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Next, a total of 65 contigs (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">13</a>) were screened out by BLAST searching analysis of the assembled genome using the sequences of GCS1−GCS5, resulting in the identification of a 7.36 Mb sequence library proposed to represent the centromeric DNA of <i>O. granulata</i>. About 45% of this library is composed of interspersed repeats, in which <i>gypsy</i>-type LTRs accounted for about 81.37%; whereas no tandem repeats were found in this library (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">14</a>), which further confirmed the centromeres of <i>O. granulata</i> are lacking tandem repeats. Further sequence analysis revealed that the Centromeric Retrotransposon of Maize (CRM) family is the major centromeric LTR sequence present in <i>O. granulata</i> (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">15</a>). Analysis of centromeric contig2720 using LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1434">30</a></sup> showed that centromere-specific LTRs of <i>O. granulata</i> are inserted into each other, and abundant LTR insert traces were found within the full-length LTRs (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM3">1</a>). Collectively, centromeres of <i>O. granulata</i> chromosomes lack tandem repeats and are primarily composed of unique sequences that are interspersed with <i>gypsy</i>-type LTRs.</p><p>4′,6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (DAPI)-stained pachytene chromosomes were further investigated to understand the characteristics of centromeres that are naturally lacking tandem repetitive sequences in <i>O. granulata</i>. We found that the centromeric region of each <i>O. granulata</i> pachytene chromosome was distinctly constricted (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#Fig3">3e</a>). This phenomenon was never observed in <i>O. sativa</i> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#Fig3">3f</a>) or in other species of the genus <i>Oryza</i> (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Repeat-driven evolution of intron size in <i>O. granulata</i>
                        </h3><p>Pairwise comparisons of exons and introns of homologous genes detection as Reciprocal Best Hits indicated that exon length is conserved among <i>Oryza</i> species, while intron length varied statistically significantly (<i>t</i> test in R package, <i>P</i> value = 7.325e-16). The average length of individual introns was found to be 378.87 bp and 415.94 bp for <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>O. granulata</i>, respectively (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">16</a> and Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">9</a>). Taken together, these data suggest that intron length increased as <i>O. granulata</i> evolved over time.</p><p>Next, we investigated whether the presence of more repetitive sequences may represent a possible origin of the long introns observed in <i>O. granulata</i>. The results showed that the expanded <i>O. granulata</i> introns identified in our study, relative to their homologous counterparts in other <i>Oryza</i> species, contain TEs (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">10</a>), indicating that insertion of TEs is the key factor driving the increase of intron size in <i>O. granulata</i> (cor.test in R package, <i>R</i> = 0.95). Sequence analysis also revealed that the largest TE insertion was classified as a <i>RIRE2</i> LTR (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">17</a>), and the expansion of <i>RIRE2</i> LTRs may specifically contribute to the observed increased of intron lengths. These data suggest that in <i>O. granulata</i>, gene length should be increased as consequence of intron expansion. Indeed, comparative analysis of length of homologous genes between <i>O. granulata</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> showed that <i>O. granulata</i> has, on average, longer genes than <i>O. sativa</i> (3.27 vs. 3.06Kb) (Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM3">1</a> and Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">11</a>). Taken together, we propose that the expansion of TEs in the intronic regions of the <i>O. granulata</i> genome directly led to the observed increased gene size.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene family evolution in <i>O. granulata</i>
                        </h3><p>We identified 18,981 gene families in <i>O. granulata</i> based on predicted protein-coding genes. More than 90% of the identified gene families (17,229) are shared by both <i>O. sativa</i> and/or <i>O. brachyantha</i> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#Fig4">4</a>). Interestingly, we identified 1752 gene families that are only present in <i>O. granulata</i>, but are absent in both <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>O. brachyantha</i>. Analysis of GO terms and InterPro domains for these lineage-specific families revealed that several functional pathways involved in DNA integration, photosynthesis, protein translation, and ATP biosynthesis are enriched in <i>O. granulata</i> (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">18</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs42003-018-0089-4/MediaObjects/42003_2018_89_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs42003-018-0089-4/MediaObjects/42003_2018_89_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="499"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Venn diagram showing the distribution of gene families between <i>O. granulata</i>, <i>O. brachyantha</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, and <i>B. distachyon</i>. The intersections between species indicate the numbers of shared gene families, while unique family numbers are shown in species-specific areas. The center represents the number of families shared by all species simultaneously. Only families with four or more protein members were considered. Analysis of families showed 12,585 families shared by all species simultaneously</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Expansion or contraction of gene families is often associated with the adaptations of plants to different environoments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. The life and death of gene families. Bioessays 31, 29–39 (2009)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1646">31</a></sup>. As compared with both of <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>O. brachyantha</i>, 420 gene families with a bigger size were expanded in <i>O. granulata</i>, whereas only two of them were found containing a few members (the size of family required at least five genes in <i>O. granulata</i>, and the number of genes from one family required twofold changes). GO and InterPro domain analyses of the expanded families indicated that those protein families associated with TEs, such as the transposase-derived protein, ribonuclease H-like domain, Aminotransferase-like, and plant mobile domain families are extremely overrepresented in <i>O. granulata</i> (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">19</a> and Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM4">2</a>).</p><p>Gene families associated with photosynthesis and ATP synthesis are also obviously expanded in <i>O. granulata</i> (Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM5">3</a> and&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM6">4</a>). We identified 154 genes encoding the components of photosystem I and II in <i>O. granulata</i>, in contrast to the presence of only 40 genes in <i>O. sativa</i> and 7 in <i>O. brachyantha</i>, respectively. We also identified 68 genes encoding ATPase, which is apparently much more than that 16 and 6 genes present in <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>O. brachyantha</i>, respectively. This expansion suggests that there has been stronger selection for enhanced light utilization and efficiency of energy production in <i>O. granulata</i>. This positive selection is consistent with the shade-requiring growth habit of <i>O. granulata</i>. In comparison with other <i>Oryza</i> species, <i>O. granulata</i> usually grows in well-drained soil under trees on small hills, and is therefore often shaded during peak daylight hours. Therefore, the expansion of these genes may play important roles in the adaptation to the lower light environment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Positive selection of <i>O. granulata</i> genes</h3><p>The seven closely related rice genomes provide a phylogenetic framework to examine protein-coding genes showing accelerated molecular evolution in rice species and detect signals of positive selection on genes involved in adaptive divergence (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#Fig5">5</a>). We identified 3746 single-copy gene families displaying accelerated evolution (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">20</a>). GO/IPR classification (Supplementary Tables&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">21</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">22</a>) showed that the detected positive selection genes (PSGs) spanned a wide range of functional categories. Notably, there is a statistically significant (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05) enrichment of 171 PSGs involved in pathways related to energy synthesis and metabolism (Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM7">5</a> and&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM8">6</a>). Of these 171 PSGs, 30 genes involved in energy synthesis and metabolism categories show evidence for positive selection. These results suggested that the function of the genes involved in photosynthesis and energy production underwent positive selection during the evolution of <i>O. granulata</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs42003-018-0089-4/MediaObjects/42003_2018_89_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs42003-018-0089-4/MediaObjects/42003_2018_89_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="270"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Phylogeny and divergence time estimation by molecular clock analysis. Divergence times of seven <i>Oryza</i> species were calculated using the first codon positions of 3746 single-copy protein-coding genes. Tree topology is supported by 100% bootstrap values. The blue ellipses on the nodes indicate the 95% credibility intervals of the estimated posterior distributions of the divergence times. The red circles indicate the fossil calibration times used for setting the upper and lower bounds of the estimates. MYA million years ago</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In addition, ten genes involved in protein translation are PSGs. Of these genes, five are associated with tRNA biosynthesis and two are involved in transcriptional elongation, suggesting the enhanced selection for translation in <i>O. granulata</i>. Although the direct reason behind an expansion of gene families involved in protein synthesis remains unclear, we speculate the large expansions observed&nbsp;in families related to photosynthesis in <i>O. granulata</i> may contribute a lot to this expansion.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The genus <i>Oryza</i> represents an excellent system for studying genomic evolution in plants. In addition to the two cultivated rice species <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>O. glaberrima</i>, the reference genomes of wild species with AA and FF genomes have been released<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Chen, J. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of Oryza brachyantha reveals mechanisms underlying Oryza genome evolution. Nat. Commun. 4, 1595 (2013)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1798">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zhang, Q. J. et al. Rapid diversification of five Oryza AA genomes associated with rice adaptation. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4954–E4962 (2014)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1801">12</a></sup>. <i>O. granulata</i> is one of the oldest wild rice species in the genus <i>Oryza</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Du, H. L. et al. Sequencing and de novo assembly of a near complete indica rice genome. Nat. Commun. 8, 15324 (2017)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1811">9</a></sup>, and its GG type genome shows obvious distinctions from both the AA and FF genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ammiraju, J. S. et al. The Oryza bacterial artificial chromosome library resource: construction and analysis of 12 deep-coverage large-insert BAC libraries that represent the 10 genome types of the genus. Oryza. Genome Res. 16, 140–147 (2006)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1815">13</a></sup>. Moreover, <i>O. granulata</i> is the only wild <i>Oryza</i> species that grows in upland habitats not in or near water. Therefore, <i>O. granulata</i> provides an excellent opportunity to explore <i>Oryza</i> genome evolution.</p><p>Here we present a ~780 Mb genome of the wild rice, <i>O. granulata</i>, using SMRT sequencing. The genome is about twice the size of <i>O. sativa</i>, which is the largest genome among the sequenced <i>Oryza</i> species so far. In plants, with the exception of polyploidization, TE proliferation is a primary mechanism leading to genome expansion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Soltis, P. S., Marchant, D. B., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Polyploidy and genome evolution in plants. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 35, 119–125 (2015)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1844">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Hawkins, J. S., Kim, H., Nason, J. D., Wing, R. A. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Differential lineage-specific amplification of transposable elements is responsible for genome size variation in Gossypium. Genome Res. 16, 1252–1261 (2006)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1847">32</a></sup>. Similarly, we showed that the expansion of the <i>O. granulata</i> genome also resulted from insertions of LTR retrotransposons. We also revealed that three subtypes of LTR families, <i>RIRE2</i>, <i>ATLANTYS</i>, and <i>Copia</i>, expanded rapidly in <i>O. granulata</i>, making a large contribution to its expanded genome size. Homologs of these three elements were found in nearly all the published <i>Oryza</i> genomes, suggesting that these elements were present in the common ancestor of the <i>Oryza</i> genus. Examination of complete sequences of these LTR retrotransposons revealed that they had undergone independent amplification around 1–3 million years ago, after the speciation of <i>O. granulata</i>. Furthermore, FISH identification analyses revealed that <i>RIRE2</i> is mainly inserted into pericentromeric regions of <i>O. granulata</i> chromosomes. Those results provide direct evidence that the activity of LTR retrotransposons may increase genome size to such a degree over a short evolutionary period.</p><p>TE amplification is a nonnegligible driving force for genome expansion and gene collinearity erosion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Alfoldi, J. &amp; Lindblad.-Toh, K. Comparative genomics as a tool to understand evolution and disease. Genome Res. 23, 1063–1068 (2013)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1886">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Piegu, B. et al. Doubling genome size without polyploidization: dynamics of retrotransposition- driven genomic expansions in Oryza australiensis, a wild relative of rice. Genome Res. 16, 1262–1269 (2006)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1889">3</a></sup>. We provided direct evidence that bursting of <i>Gypsy</i>-type LTRs is responsible for the GG genome expansion. In addition, we conducted statistical analysis of collinearity between <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>O. granulata</i>, and other four species, 1597 <i>O. granulata</i> vs. <i>O. sativa</i> syntenic gene blocks were identified, which are only comprised of 30.15% (16,875) of <i>O. sativa</i> genes (Supplementary Tables&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">23</a> and&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">24</a>). We also detected fewer collinear blocks between <i>O. granulata</i> and <i>O. nivara</i>, or between <i>O. granulata</i> and <i>O. brachyantha</i> (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">12</a>). While in the noncollinear regions, repetitive sequences are more prevalent than collinear blocks (Supplementary Figs.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">13</a> and&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#MOESM1">14</a>). These results suggest repetitive sequences might integration into noncollinear genes in <i>O. granulata</i>, leading to the erosion of gene collinearity. However, due to the lack of chromosome level assembly, more detailed analysis of gene collinearity remains to be performed.</p><p>Centromere-specific satellite repeats are intrinsic for their biological functions. In humans, alpha satellites are sufficient for artificial chromosome constructions, while neocentromeric DNA is incompetent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Saffery, R. et al. Construction of neocentromere-based human minichromosomes by telomere-associated chromosomal truncation. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 98, 5705–5710 (2001)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1946">33</a></sup>. Centromere-specific satellites are the most common feature of centromeres reported so far<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jiang, J., Birchler, J. A., Parrott, W. A. &amp; Dawe, R. K. A molecular view of plant centromeres. Trends Plant. Sci. 8, 570–575 (2003)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1950">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Melters, D. P. et al. Comparative analysis of tandem repeats from hundreds of species reveals unique insights into centromere evolution. Genome Biol. 14, R10 (2013)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1953">19</a></sup>. However, some extreme and/or exceptional cases also exist. For example, the rice Cen8 contains only ~65 kb of the centromeric repeat CentO, accounting less than 10% of the functional centromere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Nagaki, K. et al. Sequencing of a rice centromere uncovers active genes. Nat. Genet. 36, 138–145 (2004)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1957">34</a></sup>. Five potato centromeres (Cen4, Cen6, Cen10, Cen11, and Cen12) lack satellite repeats and only consist of single- or low-copy DNA sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Gong, Z. Y. et al. Repeatless and repeat-based centromeres in potato: implications for centromere evolution. Plant Cell 24, 3559–3574 (2012)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1961">20</a></sup>. Unexpected, we could not detect any tandem repeats at centromere regions of <i>O. granulata</i>, only with LTR retrotransposons. It is the single genome without any satellite repeats at centromeric regions identified in the genus <i>Oryza</i> so far. However, in the wild rice <i>Oryza punctata</i> (BB), a 165 bp CentO, containing a 10 bp insertion (TTTATAGGCA) compared with the 155 bp monomer of <i>O. sativa</i>, is the major component of its functional centromeres<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Zhang, W. L. et al. The transcribed 165-bp CentO satellite is the major functional centromeric element in the wild rice species Oryza punctata. Plant Physiol. 139, 306–315 (2005)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1978">35</a></sup>. ChIP cloning using antibodies against CENH3 also reveal that satellite repeats, including the 126 bp CentO-C1, 366 bp CentO-C2, and 154 bp CentO-F, are genome-specific centromeric elements of <i>O. rhizomatis</i> (CC) and <i>O. brachyantha</i> (FF), respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lee, H. R. et al. Chromatin immunoprecipitation cloning reveals rapid evolutionary patterns of centromeric DNA in Oryza species. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 102, 11793–11798 (2005)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1989">14</a></sup>. Southern blot hybridization using CentO, CentO-C1, CentO-C2, and CentO-F on digested genomic DNA of <i>O. granulata</i> suggests that CentO repeats have either diverged substantially or have been replaced by unrelated sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lee, H. R. et al. Chromatin immunoprecipitation cloning reveals rapid evolutionary patterns of centromeric DNA in Oryza species. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 102, 11793–11798 (2005)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39817e1996">14</a></sup>.</p><p>The presence of two extreme types of centromeres in the <i>Oryza</i> genus supports the hypothesis of a sudden, rather than gradual, evolutionary transition from tandem-repeatless to satellite repeat-based centromeres, similar to the phenomenon observed in potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Cheng, Z. K. et al. Functional rice centromeres are marked by a satellite repeat and a centromere-specific retrotransposon. Plant Cell 14, 1691–1704 (2002)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2007">22</a></sup>. Amplified DNA from retrotransposons appears to be a major source for the generation of new centromeric satellite repeats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Tek, A. L., Song, J. Q., Macas, J. &amp; Jiang, J. M. Sobo, a recently amplified satellite repeat of potato, and its implications for the origin of tandemly repeated sequences. Genetics 170, 1231–1238 (2005)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2011">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Macas, J., Koblizkova, A., Navratilova, A. &amp; Neumann, P. Hypervariable 3′ UTR region of plant LTR-retrotransposons as a source of novel satellite repeats. Gene 448, 198–206 (2009)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2014">37</a></sup>. There are abundant LTR retrotransposons inserted into each other frequently in the centromeres of GG genome. The high-frequency insertion of centromere-specific LTRs in GG genome would make some centromeres bigger than their homologous chromosomes. According to meiotic drive hypothesis, haploid gametes with a large centromeric region will increase their transmission to the egg<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Akera, T. et al. Spindle asymmetry drives non-Mendelian chromosome segregation. Science 358, 668–672 (2017)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2018">38</a></sup>. Thus we suspect that the GG genome is still on the way towards generating centromere-specific satellite repeats.
