In addition, we found the CRM families, centromere-specific LTRs of maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Nagaki, K. et al. Molecular and cytological analyses of large tracks of centromeric DNA reveal the structure and evolutionary dynamics of maize centromeres. Genetics 163, 759–770 (2003)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2022">39</a></sup>, are the most prominent components of the centromere-specific LTRs in the GG genome, suggesting that CRM families might be reserved centromeric sequences from their common ancestor. Collectively, centromere organization in the GG genome provides direct evidence that centromeres lacking tandem repeats may also stably exist and CR elements might be a key driving force during the evolution.</p><p>The expansion of gene families and accelerated molecular evolutionary rates are often associated with positive natural selection and adaptive evolution in plants. In <i>O. granulata</i>, we detected several protein-coding genes under considerable positive selection that are mainly involved in photosynthesis and energy production. These genes are thought to be responsible for adaptive evolution to the native shade-requiring environment of <i>O. granulata</i>. We also detected several other genes under the positive selection involved in translation pathways.</p><p>In summary, we generated a high-quality reference genome sequence of <i>O. granulata</i>. Comparative genomic analyses among <i>Oryza</i> genomes reveal possible mechanisms underlying genome size expansion, centromere evolution, and gene adaption, among other processes. Moreover, the gene family expansion and high evolutionary rate are well associated with the adaptation of <i>O. granulata</i> to its native environment.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant materials</h3><p>Plants of <i>O. granulata</i> (IRGC Acc. No.102117) were kindly provided by the International Rice Research Institute (IRRI) and cultivated in greenhouse under appropriate shading conditions. Panicles of Zhongxian 3037 and wild rice species, including <i>O. punctata</i> (BB), <i>Oryza officinalis</i> (CC), <i>Oryza australiensis</i> (EE), and <i>O. brachyantha</i> (FF), were kept in Yangzhou University and used for cytological studies.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Genomic DNA of <i>O. granulata</i> was extracted from young leaves with the DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen, USA). Sequencing was performed with both the PacBio SMRT and Illumina Hiseq platforms. We constructed the fosmid library consisting of about 2.5 million clones using the CopyControl™ Fosmid Library Production Kit with pCC1FOS™ Vector (Epicentre, USA).</p><p>Total RNA from roots, stems, leaves, leaf sheaths, and panicles was isolated using the TRIzol<sup>®</sup> Reagent (Thermo Fisher Scientific, USA). We then used Dynabeads with Oligo (dT) primers to enrich for mRNA. Enriched mRNA was fragmented into 200–700 nucleotide fragments and reverse-transcribed into cDNA using random hexamer primers. The products were purified with the QIAquick PCR Purification Kit, and ~250 bp fragments were used to construct the sequencing library using the mRNA-seq sample prep kit (Illumina). Sequencing was performed with the Illumina Genome Analyzer (150 bp PE).</p><p>The genome size of <i>O. granulata</i> was estimated by <i>K</i>-mer frequencies according to the Lander−Waterman theory<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Lander, E. S. &amp; Waterman, M. S. Genomic mapping by fingerprinting random clones: a mathematical analysis. Genomics 2, 231–239 (1988)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2099">40</a></sup>. The genome was initially assembled using Canu (1.0) with the PacBio reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2103">24</a></sup>, according to the following four steps: (1) Detecting overlaps in high-noise sequences using MHAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Berlin, K. et al. Assembling large genomes with single-molecule sequencing and locality-sensitive hashing. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 623–630 (2015)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2107">41</a></sup>; (2) Generating corrected sequence consensus; (3) Trimming corrected sequences, including suspicious regions, such as remaining SMRTbell adapters; and (4) Assembling trimmed corrected sequences. For Canu, the following parameters were used: errorRate = 0.01 and genomeSize = 780 Mb. Default parameters were otherwise employed for Canu assembly. We also tested other assemblers to estimate the PacBio Canu assembly results. Raw Illumina HiSeq data from three libraries of small insert sizes (&lt;800 bp) were assembled by SOAPdenovo2 with different <i>K</i>-mer size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2115">25</a></sup>. Moreover, WGS Illumina sequences from <i>O. granulata</i> DNA were used to assess the error rate of the integrated assembly and residual within-genome heterozygosity. High-quality data were aligned to the assembly using BWA mem (0.7.10)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows−Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2122">42</a></sup>, followed by application of GATK (3.2-2) to call variants with a minimum coverage threshold of ten reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2126">43</a></sup>, including SNPs and indels. We corrected homozygous SNPs and indels with at least five supporting reads. Finally, these reads were used to polish the assembly using Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2130">26</a></sup> (<a href="https://github.com/broadinstitute/pilon">https://github.com/broadinstitute/pilon</a>), a custom tool to correct remaining indel errors.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Repeat annotation</h3><p>Repeat sequences in the <i>O. granulata</i> genome were identified using the following procedures. First, tandem repeats were identified across the genome using Tandem Repeats Finder software (4.07)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeat finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acid Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2153">44</a></sup>. Next, TEs were predicted by homology-based methods using RepeatMasker (4.0.6) (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) and RepeatProteinMask, and de novo approaches using LTR_STRUCT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="McCarthy, E. M. &amp; McDonald, J. F. LTR_STRUC: a novel search and identification program for LTR retrotransposons. Bioinformatics 19, 362–367 (2003)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2164">45</a></sup>, LTR_FINDER (1.0.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2168">30</a></sup>, and RepeatScout (1.0.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, I351–I358 (2005)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2173">46</a></sup>. RepeatMasker was employed for DNA-level identification using a general library (RepBase20.04). At the protein level, RepeatProteinMask (the updated software as included in the RepeatMasker package) was employed for a WuBlastX searching against the TE protein database. LTR_STRUCT and LTR_FINDER were used to search the whole genome for characterizing structures of full-length LTR retrotransposons. RepeatScout built consensus sequences using fit-preferred alignment scores. Contamination and multicopy genes in the library were filtered first. The RepeatScout library was used for RepeatMasker. We repeated the program to identify homologs in the genome and to categorize the identified repeats.</p><p>Moreover, we estimated insertion times for the full-length LTR retrotransposons identified above. We first aligned the 5′- and 3′- LTR sequences of the retrotransposons and used these to calculate the average number of substitutions per aligned site (the <i>K</i>-value) using the EMBOSS (6.5.7.0) package distmat. Insertion times (<i>T</i>) were calculated according to the following formula: <span class="mathjax-tex">\(T\,=\,K/(2\,\times\,r),\)</span>, where <i>r</i> represents the average substitution rate, in this case 1.3×10<sup>−8</sup> substitutions per synonymous site per year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Ma, J. X. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2244">47</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">ChIP cloning</h3><p>Nuclei were isolated from young leaves according to previously published protocols with minor modification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lee, H. R. et al. Chromatin immunoprecipitation cloning reveals rapid evolutionary patterns of centromeric DNA in Oryza species. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 102, 11793–11798 (2005)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2256">14</a></sup>. We purified nuclei twice by sucrose density gradient centrifugation. ChIP experiments using the rabbit antibody against rice CENH3 were performed as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Nagaki, K. et al. Sequencing of a rice centromere uncovers active genes. Nat. Genet. 36, 138–145 (2004)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2260">34</a></sup>. Immunoprecipitated DNA was extracted with phenol/chloroform and precipitated with ethanol. The extracted DNA was converted into blunt-ended DNA fragments by filling in or trimming back 3′ and 5′ overhangs. Next, we phosphorylated 5′ ends and added A-overhangs to 3′ ends using the NEBNext<sup>®</sup> Ultra™ DNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina (NEB, USA). Modified DNAs were cloned into the pMD19-T vector (TAKARA, Japan), and clones with an insert size larger than 150 bases were sequenced.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">FISH and Immuno-FISH</h3><p>The preparation of meiotic pachytene chromosomes and subsequent FISH analysis were the same as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lee, H. R. et al. Chromatin immunoprecipitation cloning reveals rapid evolutionary patterns of centromeric DNA in Oryza species. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 102, 11793–11798 (2005)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2275">14</a></sup>. Briefly, we labeled probes from clones with inserts larger than 1000 bp with digoxigenin-dUTP. <i>RIRE2</i> probes were labeled with Biotin-dUTP. Polyclonal rabbit antibodies against rice CENH3, with a concentration of 1 μg per μL, was used to label centromeric regions on chromosomes. Chromosomes were counterstained with DAPI. Images of hybridization signals and chromosomes were captured using an Olympus BX51 fluorescence microscope system (Olympus, Japan) equipped with a micro CCD camera. Preparation for meiotic pachytene chromosomes and procedures for fluorescence immunolocalization are similar to a previously described protocol with minor modifications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Zhang, W. L. et al. The transcribed 165-bp CentO satellite is the major functional centromeric element in the wild rice species Oryza punctata. Plant Physiol. 139, 306–315 (2005)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2282">35</a></sup>. Anthers at pachytene stage were directly fixed in 4% (w/v) paraformaldehyde for 50 min at room temperature. After recording immunostaining signals, slides were washed with PBS buffer and FISH was carried out as described with the indicated probes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Centromere sequence screening and annotation</h3><p>Sequences of centromere-specific probes identified by FISH were used to screen the centromeric regions of <i>O. granulata</i>. BLAST searching of the assembled genome of <i>O. granulata</i> using sequences of centromere-specific probes was performed under the following conditions: that with the matched lengths matched greater than 1000 bp and similarity higher than 95%. A total of 65 contigs were identified, which resulted in the formation of a 7.36 Mb sequence library representing the centromeric sequences in <i>O. granulata</i>. The sequence data were consequently used for centromeric elements analysis. Repeat sequences were identified as follows: Tandem repeats were searched using Tandem Repeats Finder (4.09) (<a href="http://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html">http://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html</a>) and TEs were predicted using RepeatMasker (4.0.6) (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Gene annotation</h3><p>The <i>O. granulata</i> protein-coding gene set was inferred using de novo, homologous and evidence-based gene prediction (i.e., RNA-seq data) approaches. We performed de novo gene prediction on a repeat-masked genome using three programs, namely Augustus (3.0.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, W309–W312 (2004)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2328">48</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM (3.0.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878–2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2332">49</a></sup>, and SNAP (version 11/29/2013)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinforma. 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2336">50</a></sup>. Training models were generated from a subset of the transcriptomic data set representing 800 distinct genes. Homologous gene prediction was performed by comparing protein sequences of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (TAIR10), <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> (v1.0), <i>O. brachyantha</i> (v1.4b), <i>O. glaberrima</i> (AGI1.1), <i>Oryza indica</i> (ASM465v1), <i>O. sativa</i> (v7.0), and the uniprot sprot database (release-2015_04). For each reference, we performed the following steps. First, we predicted putative homologous genes from protein sequence alignments representing the complete gene set (the longest transcripts were chosen to represent each gene) with TBLASTN 2.2.18 (<i>e</i> value cut-off: 1e-5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2363">51</a></sup>. Second, the corresponding regions were retrieved, together with sequences 2 kb downstream and upstream of the aligned regions. Third, we further processed the alignments using GeneWise (2.2.0) to extract accurate exon and intron information<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2367">52</a></sup>. Evidence-based gene prediction was conducted through alignment of all RNA-seq data generated herein against the assembled genome using TopHat (2.0.12)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2371">53</a></sup>, with cDNAs predicted from the resultant data using Cufflinks (2.2.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Mortazavi, A., Williams, B. A., Mccue, K., Schaeffer, L. &amp; Wold, B. Mapping and quantifying mammalian transcriptomes by RNA-Seq. Nat. Methods 5, 621–628 (2008)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2375">54</a></sup>. The resulting cDNA sequences were then aligned to the <i>O. granulata</i> genome using BLAT (v. 34 × 12). Following gene prediction, a nonredundant gene set representing homologous genes, de novo genes, and RNA-seq supported genes, was generated using EVidenceModeler (EVM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2382">55</a></sup>. In addition, transposase proteins were filtered when a combination of the following conditions is true. First, the protein function was identified as transposase in swissprot or InterPro database (relevant terms include transpose, transposon, Retrotransposon, retrovirus, retrotransposon, reverse transcriptase, transposase, and retroviral). Second, the protein sequences were searched against the transposase database (BLASTP <i>e</i> = 1e-10) and sequences with matches were excluded. tRNA genes were searched for using tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2390">56</a></sup>. For rRNA identification, we downloaded <i>Oryza</i> rRNA sequences from NCBI and aligned them against the <i>O. granulata</i> genome to identify possible rRNAs. Additionally, other types of noncoding RNAs, including miRNA and snRNA, were identified utilizing INFERNAL to search the Rfam database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335–1337 (2009)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2400">57</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Functional annotation</h3><p>We performed predicted gene annotation in <i>O. granulata</i> by aligning sequences against a number of protein sequence databases including InterPro (54.0), Gene Ontology, KEGG (version 59)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Ogata, H. et al. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 29–34 (1999)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2415">58</a></sup>, Swiss-Prot (release-2015_04)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledge base and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365–370 (2003)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2419">59</a></sup>, TrEMBL (release-2015_04), and NR (07/03/2015). First, to predict protein-coding genes in the <i>O. granulata</i> genome, each inferred amino acid sequence was assessed for conserved protein domains in the gene3d, hamap, pfam, pirsf, prints, prodom, smart, superfamily, and tigrfam databases using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W116–W120 (2005)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2426">60</a></sup>. The Gene Ontology IDs for each gene were obtained from the corresponding InterPro entries. Second, amino acid sequences were subjected to BLASTP (2.2.26) (<i>e</i> value &lt; 1e-5) using the following protein databases: Swiss-Prot, TrEMBL, Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG), and NCBI protein NR.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>Phylogenetic analysis of the seven sequenced <i>Oryza</i> genomes was performed using 1:1 single-copy orthologous genes. OrthoMCL clustered a total of 3746 single-copy gene families, which were individually aligned with MUSCLE and then subjected to phylogenetic analyses by MrBbayes with using <i>A. thaliana</i> as outgroup<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Huelsenbeck, J. P. &amp; Ronquist, F. MRBAYES: Bayesian inference of phylogenetic trees. Bioinformatics 17, 754–755 (2001)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2448">61</a></sup>. These genes robustly resolved phylogenetic relationships among the five <i>Oryza</i> species genomes with all the nodes obtaining full (100%) bootstrap support. Divergence time was conducted with PAML McMcTree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Yang, Z. H. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Bioinformatics 13, 555–556 (1997)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2455">62</a></sup>, and the following constraints were used for time calibrations: 40–53 million years ago (MYA) for the <i>Brachypodium</i>-<i>Oryza</i> split<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Vogel, J. P. et al. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2466">63</a></sup> and 15–24 MYA for the origin and divergence of <i>Oryzeae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Tang, L. et al. Phylogeny and biogeography of the rice tribe (Oryzeae): evidence from combined analysis of 20 chloroplast fragments. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 54, 266–277 (2010)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2472">64</a></sup>. Divergence time dating suggested a recent radiation of about (~ 3.9 MYA) within major AA-genome lineages and a second split (~12.0 MYA) between the GG and FF genomes. Based on the estimated divergence times of the seven species and the inferred phylogenetic tree, the expansion and contraction of gene clusters were determined by a CAFE calculation (version 2.1) on the basis of changes in gene family size in generated phylogenetic history<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., &amp; Demuth, J. P., &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269–1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2476">65</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Gene collinearity</h3><p>Syntenic blocks between <i>O. granulata</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> were defined by McScan analysis based on core-orthologous gene sets identified using InParanoid (BLAST <i>e</i> value ≤ 1e -5; number of genes required to call synteny ≤5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="O’Brien, K. P., Remm, M. &amp; Sonnhammer, E. L. L. Inparanoid: a comprehensive database of eukaryotic orthologs. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D476–D480 (2005)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2497">66</a></sup>. We confirmed that syntenic blocks represented the orthologous blocks identified between <i>O. granulata</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>. We then classified genes as either collinear or noncollinear according to whether they had a homologous gene within the orthologous regions. If we failed to detect a homologous gene in the syntenic region of the target genome, we searched for DNA sequences homologous to the candidate gene within this region. We then assigned the syntenic status “without synteny” for this gene if sequence remnants were detected, indicating the orthologous gene was probably mis-annotated and the synteny status of this gene is unclear. To minimize the influence of sequence gaps on synteny analysis, we manually inspected gap-containing and gap-flanking genes to confirm their synteny status and incorporated these results into our synteny analysis. In this case, we used <i>B. distachyon</i> as the outgroup to filter candidate noncollinear genes that were found to be collinear with the outgroup.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Gene family identification</h3><p>We used a comparative analysis to examine the rate of protein evolution and the conservation of gene repertoires among orthologs in the genomes of <i>A. thaliana</i> (TAIR10), <i>B. distachyon</i> (v1.0), <i>O. brachyantha</i> (v1.4b), <i>O. glaberrima</i> (AGI1.1), <i>O. indica</i> (ASM465v1), <i>O. sativa</i> (v7.0), and <i>O. granulata</i>. First, we aligned all-to-all proteins using BLASTP (<i>e</i> value of 1e-5). Genes were then clustered using OrthoMCL (1.4) with a Markov inflation index of 1.5 and a maximum <i>e</i> value of 1e-5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2548">67</a></sup>. On this basis, all gene families were ascertained from the seven reference genomes. Furthermore, based on pairwise BLASTP alignment of <i>O. granulata</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> with <i>e</i> value of 1e-5, we used the reciprocal best method to identify orthologous genes, called reciprocal best ortholog gene pairs, which showed high similarity at the amino acid level.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Enrichment analysis</h3><p>For a given gene list, such as the <i>O. granulata</i>-specific genes or conserved genes between <i>O. granulata</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>, GO enrichment analysis was used. GOstat tests for GO terms represented by significantly more genes in a given gene set using chi-square test. Fisher’s exact test is used when expected counts are below 5, which makes the chi-square test inaccurate. The computed <i>p</i> value was then adjusted for multiple tests by specifying a false discovery rate (FDR) (<i>q</i> value &lt; 0.05) using the Benjamini−Hochberg method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate—a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. Roy. Stat. Soc. B. 57, 289–300 (1995)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2586">68</a></sup>. Similar methods were also used for IPR and KEGG enrichment analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Positive selection analysis</h3><p>We investigated the molecular evolution of protein-coding genes using the set of high confidence 1:1 orthologous genes identified in the seven <i>Oryza</i> species. We first compared the average evolutionary rates of protein-coding genes along lineages and clades of the seven species phylogeny. We estimated branch-specific <i>ω</i> values (nonsyonymous−synonymous rate ratio, <i>d</i>N/<i>d</i>S) using the codonML program in PAML (version 4.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Yang, Z. H. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2610">69</a></sup>. The two models with both a single <i>ω</i> estimated for all branches and a branch-specific <i>ω</i> for each branch were compared in a likelihood ratio test (LRT). For all measurements, codon frequencies were estimated from nucleotide frequencies at each codon position (model F3×4). In order to identify genes under positive selection, we performed CodeML and a series of LRTs to calculate the ratio of synonymous (<i>d</i>S) and nonsynonymous (<i>d</i>N) changes at each codon or on particular branches or clades of interest in the phylogeny for each orthologous family. We corrected for multiple comparisons following the method of Benjamini and Hochberg<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate—a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. Roy. Stat. Soc. B. 57, 289–300 (1995)." href="/articles/s42003-018-0089-4#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d39817e2627">68</a></sup> to estimate the appropriate <i>P</i> value threshold for an FDR of &lt;0.05. Phylogenetic relationships were determined based on the tree constructed above.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Data availability</h3><p>The raw sequencing data of RNA-seq and whole-genome have been deposited at NCBI under the BioProject accession numbers PRJNA385906 and PRJNA412686, respectively. The RNA-seq reads of roots, stems, sheaths, panicles, and leaves can be accessed under Sequence Read Archive (SRA) accession numbers SRR5527094 to SRR5527098, respectively. The whole-genome reads and fosmid sequences can be accessed under SRA accession numbers SRR6175404 to SRR6175406. The whole-genome assembly has been depositd in the Genome Warehouse in BIG Data Center under accession number GWHAAEL00000000 (<a href="http://bigd.big.ac.cn/gwh">http://bigd.big.ac.cn/gwh</a>).</p></div></div></section>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;dopt=Abstract&amp;list_uids=10588717" aria-label="View reference 6 on PubMed" rel="nofollow">PubMed</a>

P.C., F.G., and C.L. designed the data analyzing project. Z.W., R.Y., and X.Z. prepared the materials. Z.W., Y.L., and X.A. performed the fosmid library construction. Z.W., R.Y., C.L., and Y.L performed sequencing. D.F., F.G., and T.Z. performed the genome assembly and annotation. D.F., P.C., and F.G., performed repeat annotation, comparative and positive selection analysis. Z.W., R.Y., Y.L., and X.A. performed ChIP experiments. Z.W., Z.C., and C.Y. performed the cytogenetic experiments. Z.W., D.F, and T.Z. performed the centromere sequence identification and annotation. Z.W., P.C, and R.Y. drafted the manuscript. All authors read, revised and commented on the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
